Let's solve the Diophantine equation 1/p + 1/x + 1/y = 1 with p prime, x,y integers > p, and x ≤ y. Multiply by pxy: xy + py + px = pxy. Rearr: xy + py + px - pxy = 0 => xy(1-p) + p(x+y) = 0 => p(x+y) = (p-1)xy. So (p-1)xy = p(x+y). This is symmetric. We can solve for x in terms of y: (p-1)xy - p x = p y => x((p-1)y - p) = p y => x = p y / ((p-1)y - p). Since x must be integer > p, and y > x. Also we need x and y to be divisors of some N. But we can find all integer solutions. This is a typical Egyptian fraction equation. Let's find all solutions with p prime.

Now, we need to determine all possible a1 such that the sequence is infinite. The sequence is defined by a_{n+1}=f(a_n). We need that for all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors. That is, the orbit stays within the set S = {N: N has at least three proper divisors}. But note that the condition "each of which has at least three proper divisors" is part of the sequence definition; it's not that the recursion is defined only if the term has at least three proper divisors; it's that the sequence is given and satisfies that property. So we need to find all a1 for which there exists an infinite sequence (i.e., the recursion yields numbers with at least three proper divisors forever). Since the recursion is deterministic, the sequence is uniquely determined by a1. So we need to find all a1 such that for all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors. Thus, the only a1 that work so far are those that eventually reach a fixed point. But we need to determine all possible a1. The problem likely expects a simple description: all a1 that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5? But 72 is not a multiple of 6? 72 is divisible by 6, yes 72=6*12, but 12 is divisible by 4? Actually 72 is divisible by 4? 72/4=18, yes 72 is divisible by 4. So 72 is divisible by 4, yet it works. So the condition is not that a1 itself is a fixed point, but that its orbit eventually lands on a fixed point. So we need to characterize the set of numbers whose iterates eventually become fixed points. This is reminiscent of "sum of three largest proper divisors" function. Let's analyze the function f(N) more systematically. We want to find all N such that the sequence defined by a_{n+1}=f(a_n) is infinite and each term has at least three proper divisors. This is equivalent to: the orbit never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. So we need to characterize the set S of numbers that have at least three proper divisors, and then the set of numbers whose forward orbit stays in S. Since f is defined for numbers in S, we need to find all N such that f(N) ∈ S, and then f(f(N)) ∈ S, etc. So we need to find all N that never map to a number with fewer than three proper divisors.

From our observations, it seems that if N is not divisible by 3, then f(N) < N, and often quickly leads to a prime or a number with only two proper divisors. Actually 95=5*19, proper:1,5,19 sum=25, then 25 has two proper divisors fails. So indeed, numbers not divisible by 3 seem to fail. But could there be a number not divisible by 3 that maps to 6? No. So the only chance for a number not divisible by 3 to survive is if it maps to a number that is divisible by 3 and then eventually to a fixed point. But we haven't found any such number.
